<div id="review_869961033" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/869961033" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/869961033?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=amazing-characters">amazing-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer869961033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6177827628674909260">In 2010, we had to read for classes one of the Sherlock Holmes' books. My teacher gave us three options. Those options were <i>The Hound of the Baskervilles</i>, <i>A Study in Scarlet</i> and <i>The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</i>. We had to vote for the one we considered the most interesting, and the winner was THotB. Since then, I made the promise to read the entire canon... but I did it with one purpose: To get to TMoSH. Do you know why? Because in this one Holmes was supposed to die, and also because Moriarty app</span>
  <span id="freeText6177827628674909260" style="display:none">In 2010, we had to read for classes one of the Sherlock Holmes' books. My teacher gave us three options. Those options were <i>The Hound of the Baskervilles</i>, <i>A Study in Scarlet</i> and <i>The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</i>. We had to vote for the one we considered the most interesting, and the winner was THotB. Since then, I made the promise to read the entire canon... but I did it with one purpose: To get to TMoSH. Do you know why? Because in this one Holmes was supposed to die, and also because Moriarty appears.<br><br>Okay, now off to review each story.<br><br><b>Silver Blaze:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: A great horse (Silver Blaze) has just disappeared and he had a race the next day, so they need him because the bets are made and people might get angry if they don't get to see the famous horse running.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: This one is not outstanding, but it was interesting to see how Holmes deduced who did the villainy. It's not a complex one, but it's not predictable, so I really enjoyed it.<br><br><b>The Yellow Face:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: Mr. Munro is married and has a happy life until one day he sees a yellow face inside a cottage where his wife was found once. He's jealous and consults Holmes to see what he can get from his story.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: This one has a touching moment in the end, which I rather liked. Oh, and Holmes is not perfect, ladies and gentlemen. He sometimes has his deductions wrong.<br><br><b>The Stockbroker's Clerk:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: A man is offered a job, but he's suspicious as to the employer's motives. Holmes and Watson hurry to see what's behind all that.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: Ah, there's always a fool. Someone is deceived, but Holmes managed to discover who the responsible for the tricks is before it was too late. A good story, in general.<br><br><b>The “Gloria Scott”:</b> <br><br><u>Plot</u>: This is Holmes' first case, dated back to his university days. The story revolves around a ship named “Gloria Scott.”<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: Holmes first case! I really liked this story. Watson always wants to know things about his friend's past, and here's where he got his fill. Sherlock was a misanthrope back in his university days. Ah, Holmes, some people never change...<br><br><b>The Musgrave Ritual:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: This was a clever one. It rounds about a family ritual and the disappearance of two people.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: As I said, this was clever and really intriguing. Holmes' way of solving the riddle (for it is indeed a riddle) is not the usual one, so I ended up enjoying it immensely.<br><br><b>The Reigate Puzzle:</b> <br><br><u>Plot</u>: Holmes is sick, and he decides to give himself (or rather Watson pushed him) a vacation. In there, Holmes finds that there are also people who need him, so he decides to quit his vacation and solve a case for them, which is about a murder. The victim had a mysterious torn piece of paper in his hand, and it appear that this paper was very valuable.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: This one was very intriguing and thrilling. It had an intense action scene that I enjoyed deeply. There was indeed a puzzle. If you can, try to solve it before Holmes reveals the answer.<br><br><b>The Crooked Man:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: A man is dead and his wife is suspected. Holmes calls Watson (Did you hear that, people? <i>Holmes</i> calls <i>Watson</i>.) to ask him his opinion about the case and to see how everything ends.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: Damn Holmes and his ability to make complicated things simple. Why did he call Watson? Not because he needed his help, but merely to have someone to show his brilliance. But the story was interesting, and we have some more of Holmes ever-growing arrogance.<br><br><b>The Resident Patient:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: Blessington hires Dr. Trelawney as his doctor and everything is well until one day something happens to Blessington and Trelawney's new patients seem rather odd, so he gets suspicious and asks Holmes for advice.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: With each story in this collection, they grew better and better. This one was really interesting and it kept me glued to the book throughout the course of it. The answer to the problem was just as intriguing as the problem itself.<br><br><b>The Greek Interpreter:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: A greek interpreter, Melas, is hired to a job. In his workplace, some weird things happen and Melas feels there's something wrong.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: In this story, Mycroft Holmes is introduced to Watson's astonishment. See, Watson? Sherlock Holmes has a brother. Mycroft was quite an interesting character. He's just as intelligent as Holmes is, the difference being that Mycroft is kind of lazy and prefers to stay either at home or at his club. This one was a wicked and interesting story.<br><br><b>The Naval Treaty:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: An important document, a naval treaty, has just been stolen from Percy Phelps' office. There are many suspects and they all could have a motive for doing the deed.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: This is the kind of story that I tend to enjoy the most. The mystery was present during the entire narration, and it always has you thinking as to who might have stolen the document. Holmes, now please take my application as your assistant. I'm serious.<br><br><b>The Final Problem:</b><br><br><u>Plot</u>: Holmes is hunting down his great arch-enemy, professor Moriarty. Moriarty is a genius and plus, he has malice, so that obviously makes him a dangerous man and Holmes is determined to bring him to an end.<br><br><u>Thoughts</u>: The best story in this collection. Sure, why not? Seeing Holmes trying to defeat an enemy that has his same abilities was great. The ending was sad because Watson was all broken because of Holmes' “death.”<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1405736419ra/10443292.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Poor him.<br><br>...<br><br>The Sherlock Holmes' canon is one of my favorite series of all time. I read the nine books in a row without getting bored, so that should tell you something. I continue to love Sherlock and all his treats. I love his arrogance, his misanthropy, his coldness, his intelligence... everything. They make him a really interesting character, and we know he's the master of deduction.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1426804365ra/14098967.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I admit that even when my favorite collection of short stories is <i>The Adventures of Sherlock Holmes</i> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/869960998?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">my review</a>), this one was also really good. And as I said, it's one of my favorite books of all time.<br><br>When I graduate as a chemist, I'm gonna be like him, and my name is gonna be Vanessa the kick-ass, mad, gorgeous and sherlockian chemist.<br><br>Just kidding. That sounds immature and stupid as hell, but I still wanna be like him.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426804208i/14098961._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><s>He looks so badass in there.</s><br><br>Anyway, what are you waiting for if you haven't read this? GO READ IT NOW!!! What else can you possibly want from a book? There's mystery, there are murders, there's a sociopath with his assistant... The perfect formula for a book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6177827628674909260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2530292908" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2530292908" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16092014-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422940350p2/16092014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2530292908?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/16092014-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/41818023-the-memoirs-of-sherlock-holmes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16092014-paul?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16092014-paul?shelf=kiddos">kiddos</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16092014-paul?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16092014-paul?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2530292908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15560074294415359539">Overall, this is a stronger set of stories than the Adventures, mainly through Conan Doyle's attempts to humanize Holmes. That said, Conan Doyle also betrays a reliance on a couple of different themes that reappear throughout the stories, especially the too-good-to-be-true job offer that turns into a ruse for a larger crime. Story-by-story mini-reviews:<br/><br/>Silver Blaze: 4* A classic and memorable story, one of the best. <br/>The Yellow Face: 5* A great twist ending, all the more impressive for its time.<br/>-</span>
  <span id="freeText15560074294415359539" style="display:none">Overall, this is a stronger set of stories than the Adventures, mainly through Conan Doyle's attempts to humanize Holmes. That said, Conan Doyle also betrays a reliance on a couple of different themes that reappear throughout the stories, especially the too-good-to-be-true job offer that turns into a ruse for a larger crime. Story-by-story mini-reviews:<br /><br />Silver Blaze: 4* A classic and memorable story, one of the best. <br />The Yellow Face: 5* A great twist ending, all the more impressive for its time.<br />- The Stock-Broker's Clerk: 3* The Red-Headed League meets the Copper Beeches meets at least one or two other Holmes stories.<br />- The Gloria Scott: 4* Someone else pointed out its similarity to the Sign of Four, but I still really enjoyed it.<br />- The Musgrave Ritual: 4* Some nice gothic touches, although it's quite a coincidence how much Holmes' narration sounds like Watson's!<br />- The Reigate Squire: 4* Another solid mystery that surprised me with its ending.<br />- The Crooked Man: 4* A locked-room mystery that is one of several in which people seem to have trouble with brain fever and guilt. <br />- The Adventure of the Resident Patient: 4* Yet <i>another</i> story where a young person receives an offer that seems too good to be true. Yet <i>another</i> story where a person has dark secrets in their past that come back to haunt them. Still a good one though.<br />- The Greek Interpreter: 3* Brother Mycroft makes his debut, but this is one of those annoying stories where the mystery is solved just in time for the bad guys to get away yet conveniently drown in a shipwreck.<br />- The Naval Treaty: 5* The best story in the group – unique problem, complicating factors and a pretty good surprise at the end.<br />- The Final Problem: 4* Not a mystery at all, but simply the story of how Holmes meets his presumed demise at the Reichenbach Falls. The introduction of Moriarty as the mastermind of London crime despite never having been mentioned by Holmes in any previous story makes it clear he's simply a plot device. But it's still a good and compelling story for all of that.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14402856790545260877">Another Holmes, another hit? Yep it was for me. I found myself liking this collection of stories more and more the further along I read. Yes there's a formula to each of them, yes you can usually see what's coming a mile off, that or it's completely implausible. Yet, there is something about Sherlock that is captivating. Is it the writing, the relationship with Watson, the whodunnit? I'm not sure exactly, but I'll quote George Orwell on the matter of Holmes in his essay entitled 'Good Bad Books'</span>
  <span id="freeText14402856790545260877" style="display:none">Another Holmes, another hit? Yep it was for me. I found myself liking this collection of stories more and more the further along I read. Yes there's a formula to each of them, yes you can usually see what's coming a mile off, that or it's completely implausible. Yet, there is something about Sherlock that is captivating. Is it the writing, the relationship with Watson, the whodunnit? I'm not sure exactly, but I'll quote George Orwell on the matter of Holmes in his essay entitled 'Good Bad Books' - <br /><br />"How about the frankly escapist writers, the purveyors of thrills and “light” humour? How about Sherlock Holmes, Vice Versa, Dracula, Helen's babies or King Solomon's mines? All of these are definitely absurd books, books which one is more inclined to laugh at than with, and which were hardly taken seriously even by their authors; yet they have survived, and will probably continue to do so. All one can say is that, while civilisation remains such that one needs distraction from time to time, “light” literature has its appointed place; also that there is such a thing as sheer skill, or native grace, which may have more survival value than erudition or intellectual power." <br /><br />Perfectly sums it up. </span>

<div id="review_1883832387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer13230684771873546034">A collection of eleven short stories, all about Sherlock Holmes and more cases he was involved in, and regardless of the fact that I am one of the biggest Sherlock Holmes' fans, this one still didn't do its magic on me. I got bored, the stories were trivial if compared with what I've read before, and they just didn't catch me or take me by surprise. <br/>The only one I really liked was "The Final Problem", but I still don't accept that ending as a logical one.<br/></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_470321982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/470321982" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/470321982?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer470321982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14988545302053670606"><b>Another Collection of Holmesian Mysteries</b><br/>7 January 2016<br/><br/>	In a way I’m not entirely sure how I should approach this book, particularly since I generally don’t review short story collections as a whole but rather each story on its merits. Mind you, that is probably going to cause a little bit of a problem when I get around to reading the Collected Tales of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe' title='Edgar Allan Poe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Edgar Allen Poe</a>, particularly since I can’t do <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/264158.The_Raven' title='The Raven by Edgar Allan Poe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Raven</a> the injustice of lumping it together with a bunch of other stories. Then there is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32767.At_the_Mountains_of_Madness' title='At the Mountains of Madness by H.P. Lovecraft' rel='nofollow noopener'>At th</a></span>
  <span id="freeText14988545302053670606" style="display:none"><b>Another Collection of Holmesian Mysteries</b><br />7 January 2016<br /><br />	In a way I’m not entirely sure how I should approach this book, particularly since I generally don’t review short story collections as a whole but rather each story on its merits. Mind you, that is probably going to cause a little bit of a problem when I get around to reading the Collected Tales of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Edgar Allen Poe</a>, particularly since I can’t do <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/264158.The_Raven" title="The Raven by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Raven</a> the injustice of lumping it together with a bunch of other stories. Then there is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32767.At_the_Mountains_of_Madness" title="At the Mountains of Madness by H.P. Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">At the Mountains of Madness</a>, though it has been a while since I have read anything by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9494.H_P__Lovecraft" title="H.P. Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">Lovecraft</a>, and even then it was only one story, ironically ‘At the Mountains of Madness’. However, I will get around to writing about them when I finally get around to reading the books (and I might read them a short story at a time, as I did with a collections of stories by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3345.Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Joseph Conrad</a>).<br /><br />	However, the problem with Sherlock Holmes is that it is, in a way, the lack of variety in the stories. That doesn’t necessarily mean that the stories themselves are bad, it is just that pretty much, with the exception of the content, all of the stories end up being of a similar structure. In fact the novels also followed this structure as well, namely: Holmes is confronted with a problem (which takes up the first part of the story), Holmes wanders about and works it out, Holmes then spends the rest of the story explaining what happened. As it turned out this formula worked out really well, if the five collection of short stories, and four novels, are anything to go by.<br /><br />	It’s not as if these stories are unoriginal either – a lot of mystery novels that I have read basically all deal with murders, and in a way it starts to become a little dull and dry. However, while people do die in a Sherlock Holmes story, and of course you have the occasional one where the eventual victim hires Holmes due to some mystery, murder isn’t always the case. In fact, you have ones that involve missing objects, or objects that have been discovered and Holmes is attempting to locate the owner. We have another one that involves a child of a previous marriage that the mother is trying to keep a secret, or a naval treaty that has become the centerpiece of a mystery. In fact it is not the crime that is important, it is the mystery, as in some cases it turns out that no crime has actually been committed.<br /><br />	I guess that is one aspect of our human nature – we love mysteries. In fact not knowing is far more exciting than actually discovering the answer, because once we know the answer all of a sudden it ceases to be a mystery and the revelation turns out to be really boring. Mind you, it isn’t as if the revelation is boring, it is just that knowing the answer is boring. It is sort of like hunting, or even courting a future wife – it isn’t the success that is exciting, it is the journey to reach that point. Sure, not all journeys are thrilling – being stuck in economy class traveling from Singapore to Frankfurt (which takes something like 12 hours) isn’t at all exciting, especially if you have somebody behind you kicking your seat (something that fortunately I didn’t have to experience), or the guy in front of you lying his seat back as far is possible and leaving it there for the entire journey meaning that I can’t use my laptop (and that is after having the previous occupant kicked out of the seat because ‘he must has a window seat’, though as it usually turns out, the previous occupant is usually moved to business class as compensation for the inconvenience – something that has happened to me).<br /><br />	One interesting thing is that it seems to be apparent that Doyle was attempting to wind up his Holmes stories – why else would he finish the final story by having Holmes thrown off the top of a waterfall. Personally, I’m probably not surprised because while people may have enjoyed the stories, Doyle might have been having a lot of trouble coming up with new stories – writers block if you will. Otherwise, it simply might have been that he had become somewhat bored with the character and wanted to move on. The problem is that once somebody creates something that is beloved by the community, then it can be pretty hard to put it behind you. In a way it is the curse of the celebrity status – once you have become a celebrity you are no longer your own person – you are now what the media, and the fans, make you out to be, and if it turns out that you break this mould, then you run the risk of losing that status all together – while it is painful, it is also incredibly addictive. One simply cannot stop being a celebrity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14988545302053670606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8BR+PW8HfiewZ+kxpe6kZKWfqSYT37h5Q9wW0YLbK/vKMhfv1Fm3TxdMXVBSG667c8BO7t1SNh4YolBgT5jZYQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KEFKUck/bMTC/NCnvitIgVEF0A+2WP/gzEkwWWD6hRQSZyODcmGlrGXXZMZJ3kJeh1o3x3jVcYeXN3borbl3jg==')})
  }

When Emily is poisoned and the authorities are baffled, Poirot puts his prodigious sleuthing skills to work. Suspects are plentiful, including the victim’s much younger husband, her resentful stepsons, her longtime hired companion, a young family friend working as a nurse, and a London specialist on poisons who just happens to be visiting the nearby village. \n\nAll of them have secrets they are desperate to keep, but none can outwit Poirot as he navigates the ingenious red herrings and plot twists that earned Agatha Christie her well-deserved reputation as the queen of mystery.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17661076548623364702\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2448.Arthur_Conan_Doyle">About Arthur Conan Doyle</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2448.Arthur_Conan_Doyle"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1495008883p3/2448.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2448.Arthur_Conan_Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
18,662
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9364032764173889521">Sir Arthur Conan Doyle was born the third of ten siblings on 22 May 1859 in Edinburgh, Scotland. His father, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/345352.Charles_Altamont_Doyle' title='Charles Altamont Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>Charles Altamont Doyle</a>, a talented illustrator, was born in England of Irish descent, and his mother, born Mary Foley, was Irish. They were married in 1855.<br/><br/>Although he is now referred to as "Conan Doyle", the origin of this compound surname (if that is how he meant it to be understood) is u</span>
  <span id="freeText9364032764173889521" style="display:none">Sir Arthur Conan Doyle was born the third of ten siblings on 22 May 1859 in Edinburgh, Scotland. His father, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/345352.Charles_Altamont_Doyle" title="Charles Altamont Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">Charles Altamont Doyle</a>, a talented illustrator, was born in England of Irish descent, and his mother, born Mary Foley, was Irish. They were married in 1855.<br /><br />Although he is now referred to as "Conan Doyle", the origin of this compound surname (if that is how he meant it to be understood) is uncertain. His baptism record in the registry of St Mary's Cathedral in Edinburgh gives 'Arthur Ignatius Conan' as his Christian name, and simply 'Doyle' as his surname. It also names Michael Conan as his godfather.<br /><br />At the age of nine Conan Doyle was sent to the Roman Catholic Jesuit preparatory school, Hodder Place, Stonyhurst. He then went on to Stonyhurst College, leaving in 1875.<br /><br />From 1876 to 1881 he studied medicine at the University of Edinburgh. This required that he provide periodic medical assistance in the towns of Aston (now a district of Birmingham) and Sheffield. While studying, Conan Doyle began writing short stories. His first published story appeared in "<i>Chambers's Edinburgh Journal</i>" before he was 20. Following his graduation, he was employed as a ship's doctor on the SS Mayumba during a voyage to the West African coast. He completed his doctorate on the subject of tabes dorsalis in 1885.<br /><br />In 1885 Conan Doyle married Louisa (or Louise) Hawkins, known as "Touie". She suffered from tuberculosis and died on 4 July 1906. The following year he married Jean Elizabeth Leckie, whom he had first met and fallen in love with in 1897. Due to his sense of loyalty he had maintained a purely platonic relationship with Jean while his first wife was alive. Jean died in London on 27 June 1940.<br /><br />Conan Doyle fathered five children. Two with his first wife—Mary Louise (28 January 1889 – 12 June 1976), and Arthur Alleyne Kingsley, known as Kingsley (15 November 1892 – 28 October 1918). With his second wife he had three children—Denis Percy Stewart (17 March 1909 – 9 March 1955), second husband in 1936 of Georgian Princess Nina Mdivani (circa 1910 – 19 February 1987; former sister-in-law of Barbara Hutton); Adrian Malcolm (19 November 1910–3 June 1970) and Jean Lena Annette (21 December 1912–18 November 1997).<br /><br />Conan Doyle was found clutching his chest in the hall of Windlesham, his house in Crowborough, East Sussex, on 7 July 1930. He had died of a heart attack at age 71. His last words were directed toward his wife: "You are wonderful." The epitaph on his gravestone in the churchyard at Minstead in the New Forest, Hampshire, reads:<br /><br />STEEL TRUE<br />BLADE STRAIGHT<br />ARTHUR CONAN DOYLE<br />KNIGHT<br />PATRIOT, PHYSICIAN &amp; MAN OF LETTERS<br /><br />Conan Doyle's house, Undershaw, located in Hindhead, south of London, where he had lived for a decade, had been a hotel and restaurant between 1924 and 2004. It now stands empty while conservationists and Conan Doyle fans fight to preserve it.<br /><br />A statue honours Conan Doyle at Crowborough Cross in Crowborough, where Conan Doyle lived for 23 years. There is also a statue of Sherlock Holmes in Picardy Place, Edinburgh, close to the house where Conan Doyle was born.<br /><br /><b>Series:</b><br />* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49996-sherlock-holmes" rel="nofollow noopener">Sherlock Holmes</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9364032764173889521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
